# The Data Science Of Healthcare Medicine And Public Health

> Data analytics plays an increasingly pivotal role in how we go about managing our healthcare systems and personal health. This nontechnical course explores a variety of ways to apply data science to medicine and public health. Data scientist Barton Poulson discusses the ways that dramatically increased quantities of data—from genetic testing, genealogy, brain scans, and other sources—are changing 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health) | Beginner | 41K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

## Skills Covered

- Public Health
- Data Science
- Healthcare Industry

## Table of Contents

### Introduction

#### Healthcare and data science
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Barton] There's more to data science than categorizing spam emails.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=4)** In fact, data science is an enormously powerful set of tools in the life and death matters of health and medicine.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=12)** I'm Barton Poulson, and in this course, we'll take a non-technical, conceptually oriented look at how data science can be effectively used in the field of healthcare, medicine, and public health.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=22)** We'll explore the ways that the principles and practices of data science can be used to help healthcare professionals predict risks for illnesses and epidemics, take proactive and preventive measures, better understand the nature of illness, and develop exciting new ways to treat diseases.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=39)** Data science can even help make healthcare more accessible and more affordable and give people a greater voice in their own wellbeing.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=46)** We'll also talk about how the COVID-19 pandemic has affected the ways in which data is used in healthcare.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=53)** I'll mention some of the important procedures that are used in data science, but more importantly, we'll explore how those ideas are used in healthcare.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=61)** Because this is a non-technical overview, anyone with an interest in healthcare, regardless of their technical background, can get important ideas and insights from this course.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/healthcare-and-data-science-29600863?u=76281980&t=70)** And so, let's get started with the data science of healthcare, medicine, and public health.

### 1. Connecting Data Science

#### Data science and COVID-19
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now, one of the challenges about talking about data science and how it connects to healthcare and medicine and public health at this point in time is that you simply can't do it without prominently addressing right up front COVID-19 and the global pandemic that resulted.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=20)** The reason for that is, as everybody who has lived through it and that's everybody, COVID has impacted pretty much everything everywhere and, as it turns out, that data science is no exception to this.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=33)** There are a few places where data science was really important and very public.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=38)** One place, for instance, was in tracking COVID.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=41)** It turns out that tracking diseases is difficult, especially with things that have a long incubation time.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=46)** And so there was a lot of work that went into tracking COVID, as well as mapping the prevalence of the disease across places and across time, and so people saw maps like this on a regular basis.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=60)** Also, the extraordinary work that went into developing vaccines in earth-shattering time and distributing the vaccines, producing them, and trying to persuade people to take them.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=72)** Data science has been involved in a substantial part of all of these.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=76)** But these are really the most obvious most public ways.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=80)** There are a few other ways that data science and COVID have interacted.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=85)** One, for instance, is data in the media.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=88)** People got used to looking at charts, especially getting used to looking at logarithmic growth charts, which under normal circumstances are confusing.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=97)** But when you're looking at something that grows exponentially like an infectious disease, that's allowed people to compare their growth rate across time from one place to another.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=106)** Also, people got more accustomed to looking at rates and not just the total number of infections or deaths in a place, but the infections or deaths per 100,000 residents, a way of making more valid comparisons.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=120)** Also, people became more aware of the tracking that is done passively on them.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=125)** It's been going on for years and they may have had some idea of it, but because tracking became so important to monitoring the disease and warning people when they've been exposed, people became more keenly, explicitly aware of tracking in cell phones, in social media and other forms of technology.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=145)** And then finally there was the development of COVID-related apps, that things that allowed you to do some kind of self-diagnosis, find out where you could get tested, qualifying for tests, getting your test results, doing contact tracing, having your vaccine passport and so on.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=160)** These are all different ways that data science contributed in one way or another to the global response to COVID-19.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=169)** There are a few other ways that have been unexpected.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=171)** One is that a lot of data scientists who work, for instance, for private companies have had to start acting like in-house epidemiologists, not so much that they're trying to find where the disease came from, but they're trying to predict the disease patterns.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=186)** So they know how it affects their company.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=188)** Say, for instance, you're in transportation, it's going to have a huge impact.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=191)** Say, you're in manufacturing.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=192)** You're concerned about the supply chain.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=193)** Say, you're in restaurants or hospitality.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=196)** You are going to have people developing their own models in your company of how COVID is affecting your business and how you can best react to it.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=205)** Next is disrupted analyses.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=207)** One of the most interesting results of this is because the pandemic created such a different data environment from anything else that in recent history, it meant that predictive models that developed using a whole host of machine learning algorithms before the pandemic were no longer valid.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=225)** They couldn't adapt necessarily to the new circumstances.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=229)** And so a number of organizations were taking all of their predictive work, putting it off to the side.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=235)** And in fact, just going back to descriptive models, just tell us what we're dealing with right now.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=239)** And instead of trying to project five years into the future, let's try to project five days or even five hours.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=246)** And it changed the way that analyses were conducted both during the pandemic and the way people are preparing for them afterwards, because they understand that there can be these huge disruptors within the data and the modeling.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=260)** It requires you to have a different method.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=262)** And actually that leads to the idea that a number of companies now are involved in what's called disaster modeling.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=269)** The idea is we may have another pandemic.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=272)** We may have another global or local emergency, but people are understanding that they need to try to be prepared for these future disruptions.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=280)** Some companies are already very good at that.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=282)** Developing a plan B for their supply chain, but this is something that more people are becoming aware of, especially when it comes to making your long-term projections, which is one of the important goals of data science work.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=295)** A few other places where the pandemic has affected data science includes things, like understanding political participation.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=303)** So COVID affected the ability for people to participate in public rallies or maybe even protests, but it didn't affect all regions or all groups equally.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=312)** Some places had higher infection rates.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=315)** Some places had groups that were less hesitant to go out, even when there was an infection.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=321)** And that makes it harder to estimate support for candidates or positions.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=325)** Also, it created this huge surge in voting by mail and absentee ballots.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=330)** And that affects dramatically the way that political predictions are made.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=337)** There's also an issue of crime and causality.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=340)** Estimating crime is hard, but understanding the causes of changes in crime is always a difficult task under the best of circumstances.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=348)** But with COVID, there were so many confounding changes, other things that could be explanations for any observed changes in crime rates.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=358)** For instance, there were lockdowns, which made it very difficult for people to move around.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=362)** Left a lot of people at home, a lot of people became unemployed and there were very serious financial problems that occurred.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=369)** A lot of people had children at home all day, or schools were empty.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=372)** There were so many different things that affected the potential exposure to crime, the motivations for crime.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=380)** And it's hard to know whether COVID caused these things or they created some of the other changes.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=387)** And it turns out that no matter what kind of analyses you're doing, teasing causality out of observational data is always difficult.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=395)** And then when it comes to something as simple as school attendance, the idea normally is you just count the number of heads in class, but we know that when schools basically everywhere when online or people were going at home, or you went to these, some people can do hybrids, some people don't have to.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=412)** It made even counting attendance, which affects things like, obviously, learning and students ability to take standardized tests, a lot of which got abandoned, but it affects local politics.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=425)** And all of that was affected by massive shifts in the pandemic.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=428)** And so some of these data questions, even something as simple as counting how many students are in fact attending school or learning from school became much more complicated by the pandemic.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=440)** Now I'm going to specifically focus on the health-related elements of data science and how it relates to things like the pandemic and other diseases and other health issues.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-covid-19?u=76281980&t=452)** But I want to address that major issue, the elephant in the room, about the pandemic before we move on to any of the rest of this to give you an idea of the context in which the recent developments in data science and health have taken place.

### 2. Researching Diseases and Populations

#### Shared history of health and data visualization
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now I know that we're all here to learn about modern technology and healthcare, but it helps if we have a very short history lesson first.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=10)** And there's two people I want to introduce to you, although, you're probably familiar with both of 'em already.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=16)** One is John Snow and the other is Florence Nightingale, both from England in the 1800s.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=23)** First off, John Snow.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=25)** He was an English physician, a medical doctor, and he lived from 1813 to 1858.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=33)** And he was a leader in the medical hygiene movement, as well as the use of anesthesia.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=40)** And he's usually considered the founder of modern epidemiology.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=45)** And a lot of this can be traced back to a single episode, that there was an outbreak of cholera in London in 1854, and he had the insight to find out what was going on and come up with a rather simple solution.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=61)** And what he did is he created this map you see here on the right.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=65)** It's a map of the area where people were dying in London, and he put a little mark for each person who died in their physical location.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=74)** Let's zoom in on that a little bit.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=77)** And what you see here is right in the middle is BROAD STREET and you see an awful lot of black lines representing a lot of deaths right there.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=86)** And also, right at the corner of Broad Street and Cambridge Street, he has marked a pump.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=92)** This was a public water pump, and unfortunately, the water was bad, but is what people used for drinking water, so they would pump the water, they would drink it, they would get cholera, and many of them would die.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=105)** The solution was really simple.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=106)** He took the handle off the pump, so it was no longer possible to pump water from it.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=111)** And that according to most versions of the story contributed to the sudden decrease of cholera in this particular outbreak.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=120)** Also, it was a fabulous way of using visual analytics and publicly available data to solve an important healthcare issue.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=130)** The next person I want to talk about is Florence Nightingale, who was an English nurse from 1820 to 1910.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=138)** Now, in addition to being a nurse, she was a social reformer, she was a statistician, she was a person of tremendous influence and is also considered the founder of modern nursing.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=148)** And one of her best known projects was an analysis of mortality or causes of death for British soldiers in Crimea.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=155)** In this one, she's simply referring to it as the Army in the East.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=160)** Now, Nightingale called this particular kind of graph a Coxcomb, but it's more commonly known as a Polar Area Diagram, or, in honor of her, a Nightingale Rose Diagram.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=171)** And it shows that acute infectious diseases, what she called Preventable or Mitigable Zymotic diseases, which are shown in blue, accounted for a much larger number of deaths than wounds, which are in red, or all other causes, which are in black.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=187)** And what this let you know is that, well, the risks of warfare, the actual injuries incurred are one thing, but it was the healthcare and the infections that resulted which were a much more significant issue.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=201)** Now, this work and other related projects that she did had a major influence on hygiene in the United Kingdom and elsewhere.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=210)** And her work was a substantial part of the massive improvement in life expectancy between 1874 and 1935.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=219)** Also, she really is one of the founders of data visualization, which is a major part of data science as a way of exploring data.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=227)** In fact, the Data Visualization Society has a journal they published that is simply called "Nightingale," in honor of her contributions, tracing back primarily to this chart.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=236)** Now, I give you this historical context of John Snow and Florence Nightingale of letting you know that the use of data driven methods, and in this case, data visualization methods, to answer questions about healthcare, medicine, and public health have a long and important history.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/shared-history-of-health-and-data-visualization?u=76281980&t=256)** And it's in that context that the rest of our presentations take place.

#### Measuring health and disease
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order to do data science with health data, you first have to measure things, so you have something to work with.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=7)** Now, when it comes to health data, the things that people are most familiar with are things, for instance, like maps, that show outbreaks of COVID or other diseases in their country, around the world, and the kind of statistic people are most accustomed to looking at, for instance, is mortality or death, usually expressed as the number of deaths per 100,000, and that's important because if you simply look at raw frequencies, countries with more people will always have more deaths, but they may not necessarily be more dangerous or risky places, and so it's important to make that adjustment.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=41)** Also, some tables of mortality can adjust for age, where they look at the average mortality rate, say, for instance, of infants, and then things are different for young children and adolescents and adults, and then it might change again for the very elderly, and the important thing about mortality is you want to contrast it or compare it with morbidity, which has to do with illness, but not death perse, and so mortality may be the most common and the most obvious measure, but there are attempts to look at other measures of health, not just are you alive or are you not?
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=76)** But say, for instance, are you sick?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=78)** And that's where you get to something called YLD, which stands for either years lived with disability or years lost to disability, and what this is, is an attempt at quantifying the effects of living with disabilities.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=92)** Say, for instance, you may be alive, but maybe you are in the hospital, and you're hooked up to IVs, and the things that you can do are restricted.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=101)** Also, this is another measure, like mortality, that may be weighted higher for younger adults than infants and elderly adults, depending on your ability to recover or to function, even with a particular illness but in addition to mortality, YLD or years lived with disability is another very common measure of health, and the third one I want to show you.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=122)** There are many more choices than that, but the third one I want to show you is sometimes called DALY, which is disability-adjusted life years, and what this is, is a sum of the years lost, both to early death and years lost to disability.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=138)** So years lost to early death.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=140)** You remove those entirely and years lost to disability, you get a fraction of those years, and DALY is one form of what's called a health-adjusted life year, or HALY.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=152)** Another common one is the quality-adjusted life years, or qualy or QALY, depending on who you ask, and so what these are, are different ways of assessing, not just are you alive or are you not?
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=164)** But looking at a very simple measure of the quality of life or the ability to live fully.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=171)** Now, a few things to keep in mind.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=173)** Number one is of course is, is no measure is perfect, and the limitations of YLD, the years lived with disability and the DALY, disability-adjusted life years, everybody recognizes these are far from perfect.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=187)** They're very large-scale overview measures, but they do function as a way of getting a very big picture.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=195)** They're good for describing, not individuals, but populations.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=199)** So again, use them for very large-scale things.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=203)** The nice thing about them, aside from.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/measuring-health-and-disease?u=76281980&t=206)** It's easier to deal with the numbers when you're able to take many different things and combine them down, sometimes, into a single number, is it makes it easier to compare the relative health of populations, both by time, see how they change historically, and by place, from one place to another.

#### Researching diseases in populations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I mentioned in another video how in 1854 the English physician John Snow was able to pinpoint this source of London's cholera outbreak that year, and it was a public water pump on Broad Street, by charting the locations of the deaths.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=17)** This is what he showed right here.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=19)** And this was a lifesaving development in using data to understand diseases in populations.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=26)** Fortunately, we don't have to get out a map and paper and pencil to find out what's going on in our health data.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=34)** There's now at least a potentially easier way to do that, and that's with what are called EHRs.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=41)** That stands for electronic health records, or sometimes called electronic medical records.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=46)** And they were first used at the Mayo Clinic in Rochester, Minnesota, in the 1960s.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=52)** That is the earliest precursor to the modern EHRs.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=58)** Now in the United States, the federal government created the Office of the National Coordinator for Health Information Technology in 2004.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=67)** And at the same time, they created a goal for most Americans to have electronic health records within the next 10 years, by 2014.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=76)** Not coincidentally, the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act required the, quote, meaningful use, of EMRs in 2014.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=86)** That is, if a doctor or a hospital or facility wanted to get reimbursed by Medicaid or Medicare.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=94)** So that's a very strong motivator 10 years after the original act.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, the nice thing about this is EHRs have opened the possibilities for research with large standardized data sets, in that for most people, you go to the doctor, you get something from the pharmacy, you get a procedure done, it's going to go into an EHR, which should have your unique identifiers.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=116)** And so it's an extremely large and diverse population.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=120)** That makes it possible to do a lot of really important research with this.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=125)** On the other hand, it's important to remember that because this is medical data, that research must comply with HIPAA regulations.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=132)** That's the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996, at least here in the United States, which places some important limits on applications or research with medical records.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=144)** Mostly it has to be anonymized in particular ways and it cannot be circulated.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=148)** There are important restrictions.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=151)** So there is both this huge amount of data, but there are critical limits on it.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=157)** On the other hand, this didn't prevent people from getting very creative about how they get their data about health, illness, disease.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=165)** So one of the most interesting versions of this is people learning that you can get health-related information from the way that people use search engines like Google or Bing or Baidu and social media like Facebook and Twitter and Instagram.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=182)** Now, there are a couple of interesting things about this.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=184)** Number one is it's basically public data.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=187)** Anybody can go onto Google Trends and get search information, or they can hook up to the Twitter API and they can download stuff.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=195)** It doesn't require going through anything near the same kind of safeguards or gatekeeping that goes about medical data.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=201)** That's both a blessing and a curse, but there's another interesting thing about it.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=205)** And that is the data is unprompted.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=207)** It's like somebody writing their own journal or talking to a friend.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=211)** They're not responding to standardized questions that have to be filled in boxes on a patient satisfaction survey or on a insurance reimbursement form.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=220)** It's them asking about their own things.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=222)** And especially when it's searched, it's totally private.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=225)** It's just between them and the computer.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=228)** The other interesting thing about this is you can get massive, massive amounts of data, the waterfall, the deluge of data, and you can get it in near real time around the globe.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=240)** So those are very important considerations.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=244)** And when you take those, you get some interesting possibilities.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=248)** Now, perhaps the best known project to build on all of this was Google Flu Trends, which was released in 2008, just in time for the H1N1 pandemic in 2009.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=260)** The approach was fundamentally simple.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=264)** Could searches for flu-related symptoms, like, I have a runny nose, I have a fever, at one point in time be used to predict diagnosed cases of the flu at a later point in time.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=274)** And at first the results were very promising.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=277)** Google was able to provide highly accurate predictions for many different locations substantially sooner and updated more frequently than say the U.S. Centers for Disease Control could do with their standard methods.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=290)** So it opened up the possibility of getting this epidemiological data from this nonmedical source.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=300)** Eventually, however, the predictions started to wander off from the true values to the point where Google stopped publishing the project in 2015, although historical data is still available and current data are available for, quote, declared research purposes.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=315)** So you would have to apply to Google and explain what you're doing.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=320)** Now, Google Flu Trends may not exist anymore, but there are other things that have been going on.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=327)** This idea was too compelling to just let it go.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=330)** So many other people and organizations have tried other approaches.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=335)** So for instance, they've tried using other additional data sources, things like Wikipedia searches or Twitter feeds or Baidu or Instagram or the connections in social networks or cell phone mobility data or remote sensing data, all sorts of different things that are available.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=352)** They've also tried a wider range of diseases.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=354)** So not just influenza-like illnesses, there's COVID of course, but also many infectious diseases like Ebola and Zika and HIV, dengue fever, and so on.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=365)** And probably one of the most productive approaches has been combining data sources and specifically including experts.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=373)** So tech-based machine learning approaches are helpful.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=376)** They are very helpful, but it's critical to include data from practicing physicians and from career epidemiologists who have a wealth of domain specific expertise to bring in, to help best tailor these mechanisms for using this data, combining it with things that maybe you can get from EHRs to get a more complete and more accurate picture.
>
> **[6:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=398)** Think of it as a form of ensemble modeling, a common approach in machine learning.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=403)** Now, this is a good time to be talking about this, 'cause I want to mention for those of you who are interested in reading research articles, the Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society A: Mathematical, Physical and Engineering Sciences.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=418)** That's important because there's a B also.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=421)** This is a research journal and it's usually just called Philosophical Transactions A.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=427)** On January 10th of 2022, had a special issue on data science approaches to infectious disease surveillance.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=435)** It's exactly what we're talking about.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=437)** And it looks at a wide range of diseases and some cutting-edge methods of taking some of these public data sources and some of the professional data and using, again, very sophisticated analytical and machine learning approaches to try to figure out how common diseases are and the patterns they have of moving through populations.
>
> **[7:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=458)** So this is a possibility for data science to still make major, major contributions.
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/researching-diseases-in-populations?u=76281980&t=463)** It turns out it's not quite as simple as waving the magic wand to Google, but there is still a gargantuan amount of data out there that can be used productively to help guide some of these public health decisions.

>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/genetic-data-and-healthcare?u=76281980&t=112)** On the other hand, when you get the data from a gene sequencer, it doesn't give you just these 700 megabytes of three million base pairs.

>

### 3. Researching Treatment and Outcomes

>

>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=272)** Number one is when you have difficult data.
>

>

>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=285)** One possibility, for example, is looking for evidence of the early stages that lead to Alzheimer's disease.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=291)** It's hard to tell from where brain image, especially before it's become severely developed.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=296)** However, researchers have been using convolutional neural networks, which are well-adapted to visual based things, like image analysis, to predict the progression of mild cognitive impairment up to more serious Alzheimer's.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=313)** Also, a place where data science methods can come in really handy is in terms of expanding resources or reach.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=320)** For several years now, there's been concern about the worldwide shortage of pathologists, especially in poorer countries.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=326)** Machine learning can't replace the valuable work that pathologists do, but algorithms can help make their work more efficient and help them to provide their critical insights to more people and more places.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=338)** A different principle applied to the machine learning based mental health chat bots that served really important functions during the pandemic when anxiety and depression spiked for huge numbers of people.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=350)** They don't replace trained therapists, but they make it possible for people who are dealing with these disorders to get started on understanding what they're dealing with, to get started on self-care, and hopefully link them to trained professionals who they can then meet either in person or online.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=368)** And then the third place where these algorithms come in helpful is in getting new insights on diagnosis.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=375)** If there's one thing we know about neural networks, and for instance, the task of image classification, is that these algorithms look at pictures in profoundly different ways than people do, using what sometimes seems like invisible clues, or maybe even they feel irrelevant to differentiate between objects.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=394)** And while this approach can make their machinations kind of inscrutable, they can also lead to new insights, especially when thinking about something like text, where particular word frequencies or patterns can be predictive, for example, of certain difficult to diagnose mental illnesses.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=413)** So there's a lot of potential for application in terms of reading very complex multi-level data for making work more efficient and allowing professionals to provide services to more people, and giving new kinds of insights for the diagnosis of diseases and illnesses.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=432)** But with that in mind, it's important to proceed with care.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=436)** It's not possible in this course, in this video, for me to even scratch the surface of the work being done here.
>
> **[7:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=442)** I imagine that for any illness you can think of, many teams are actively pursuing ways to apply data science to it from relatively simple decision trees to complex artificial neural networks.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=455)** There's tremendous promise.
>
> **[7:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=457)** At the same time, the insight, expertise, and judgment of trained medical professionals cannot be replaced.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/diagnosing-diseases?u=76281980&t=465)** The algorithms can give important clues, they serve as adjuncts, they can find obscure patterns and offer recommendations, but for now, they remain tools to aid, not replace doctors, nurses, and other caregivers in their life saving work.

#### Drug (re)discovery
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One of the most extraordinary things about the COVID pandemic was how fast vaccines were developed.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=6)** The first known cases of COVID were identified on December 12th, 2019, just under one year later, on December 8th, 2020, the first COVID vaccination was administered in the UK.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=18)** A few days later, on December 11th, 2020, in the US, the food and drug administration gave its first emergency use authorization for a COVID vaccine.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=29)** A few things contributed to this really astounding success.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=33)** Number one, of course, is that this was a global effort.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=36)** Labs around the world were devoting enormous resources to this project.
>

>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=66)** Although, in both cases, they didn't get to the vaccine stage, because the crisis had passed before the work was completed, but it made it possible to take this related work and try to adapt it to finding a COVID vaccine.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=80)** On the other hand, the important thing to remember is that this is not how things normally work.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=84)** In most cases, drug development is much, much, much slower.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=91)** So a few things to keep in mind, in many cases, the time it takes to go from initial conceptualization to final FDA approval can be 10 years or even more, during which time your lab turns into ancient ruins.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=105)** The cost for the entire process can exceed a billion dollars.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=109)** I've seen estimates of up to 2.6 billion dollars for the development of a single FDA approved drug.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=116)** And then there's a very low success rate.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=118)** 90% of projects fail, that is maybe only 10% of the medications that are first proposed, make it all the way through all the steps to get FDA approval.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=128)** So this is very, very difficult, time consuming, expensive and not guaranteed work.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=138)** And all those numbers for drug discovery are based on the assumption that someone's actually trying to find a medication for a particular condition.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=145)** When it comes to rare diseases, on the other hand, the financial calculus makes things much more challenging.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=150)** In the US, the Genetic and Rare Diseases Information Center of the National Institutes of Health defines a rare disease as one that affects less than 200,000 people in the US, and that's about less than a 10th of a percent of the population.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=163)** Curiously, there are over 7,000 known rare diseases, and so, while each one is individually uncommon, they have a significant collective impact.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=173)** In fact, it's estimated that about 10% of the US population has one rare disease or another.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=178)** That's 30 million people in the US.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=181)** But less than 5% of rare diseases actually have FDA approved treatments.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=187)** Now, one creative solution to this problem, for rare diseases or when a new pandemic arises, is to recycle, and that is to use things that have already gone through the process.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=200)** Now, I'm not talking about using household cleaners to treat infectious diseases, but rather taking medications that have already received FDA approval, that have already been through that entire process, and using them to treat other related illnesses.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=216)** Now there's a few things that work into this.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=219)** One is the relatively common practice of off-label use.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=223)** This is when a drug is prescribed to treat something other than what the FDA approved it for.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=227)** This is legal and it's common practice.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=229)** In fact, as many as one in five prescriptions are off-label.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=233)** So, that's one possibility.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=235)** On the other hand, that tends to be done in an ad hoc manner.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=239)** People know that a drug works for a particular disorder, so they give it to people, but it doesn't really build into the database.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=246)** And that's why for instance, some researchers have proposed what's called a DRUP Trial, that stands for Drug Rediscover Protocol.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=253)** To oversimplify, is a systemized recorded testing.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=258)** Think of a clinical trial of off-label use of medications.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=262)** In this case, for cancer treatment.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=263)** So the idea is, let's not just have people doing this, you know, one case at a time, but let's take this off-label use and let's admit it to a full, deliberate, explicit recording process that can contribute to the larger body of knowledge.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=278)** And then there's this last one, AI-Enabled Discovery.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=282)** This is where the data science approach comes in, where machine learning algorithms analyze the results of millions of automated trials.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=291)** These algorithms look at the cellular samples and evaluate things like their roundness, the thickness of their membrane, the shape of their mitochondria.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=299)** They then use this data, which is based on image analysis, in many cases, to build models that can massively accelerate drug discovery.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=310)** Several companies are working on versions of this approach and it has shown tremendous possibilities.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=315)** And so when it comes to drug discovery, the normal approach, which is useful, but also is expensive, time consuming and often doesn't work, there are opportunities to expand it, to facilitate the process, both through careful recording of off-label use, but also this possibility of AI-Enabled Discovery.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/drug-re-discovery?u=76281980&t=338)** There's so much work going on there, that anything I say here would be outmoded in a week or two, but it does let you know that there is very active work in applying data science, machine learning and AI to the process of drug discovery and finding ways of using existing approved medications and for solving other widespread diseases in the future.
