#### Predicting outcomes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When a doctor makes a diagnosis and chooses a treatment plan for their patient, they're relying on an enormous amount of training and experience and insight.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=9)** They're trying to predict the methods that will give the best possible outcomes for the people in their care.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=16)** But in addition to predicting the outcomes based on clinical experience and expertise, there are many other health related outcomes that can be predicted with different kinds of data.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=24)** So for example, it is possible to predict at least some of the precursors of heart risks like heart attacks based on current heart rate data on a smart watch.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=35)** Also, it's possible to predict outbreaks to some degree precision based on changes in things like the mosquito population, or if you run a medical testing lab, you'll be interested in predicting the demand for lab tests in the near and distant future to help with staffing questions.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=53)** All of these are different forms of predictive analytics applied to questions in healthcare, medicine and public health.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, there are a couple of different ways of thinking about what I mean by predictive analytics or predictive modeling when applied to healthcare.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=70)** The first one, which seems obvious is future events.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=75)** That is, this is the common use of prediction, saying what's going to happen in the future, or at least you're trying to use current observations to predict lightly outcomes at a later point in time with a certain probability, if certain conditions are met.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=88)** So you might, for instance, be trying to predict disease outbreaks based on the mosquito populations I mentioned that falls into this category as does predicting imminent depressive orders based on a person's social media posts.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=99)** Again, there is some association.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=102)** But in addition to predicting things in the future, which is what we think of as prediction within the machine learning world, there's also a huge amount of work done on what you might call counterfactuals.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=113)** And what that means is what would the response be from a different person or a different method, that is you're trying to use data from one method to approximate estimate what the outcome would be using another alternative method.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=126)** So for instance, when a smartwatch tells you that you have an irregular heartbeat, it's not predicting something in the future, it's talking about right now.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=134)** But it's attempting to tell you what a doctor might say if they were listening to your heart at the same time.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=140)** It's not that different from when your email system predicts, whether particular metrics is spam.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=144)** Not looking into the future, just trying to guess how you would categorize the message if you were to read it yourself and the same thing is widely used when applying data science in the healthcare world.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=155)** Another way to think about this is to ask whether time is included in the model.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=160)** I know it may sound strange to think of it that way, but it's not included explicitly in every model.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=165)** So for instance, in predictive analytics, there are many different models or statistical frameworks where time may or may not be included.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=175)** You can include it if you want to, but you don't have to.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=178)** That's true for most regression and correlation models.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=180)** It's true for cluster analysis.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=183)** It's true for what's called market basket analysis that is often used where if a person buys this, what's the probability that they'll also buy this.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=191)** In healthcare, it's the idea if a person has this symptom, what's the probably they also have this one or if they get this treatment, what's the probability they'll also get that one.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=200)** You can also have decision trees and random forests, which are very useful and often easy to interpret methods of analyzing data or even neural networks in deep learning where time is not necessarily included but you're trying to simply say, how would an expert look at this the same way?
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=218)** And then there are more traditional methods where time is included, where it's an inherent part of the method that includes time series analysis or growth curve modeling or survival analysis.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=230)** Also things like sequence mining, cross log analysis, and experimental designs, at least once it include some kind of before after or repeated measures assessment.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=240)** The thing to keep in mind is that when you're doing predictive modeling of either kind, is that any measured association can be used for prediction even if it doesn't have a causal relationship.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=252)** If you can measure it, you can see whether it's associated with your outcome as a predictor.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=257)** The risk, however, is that many of the associations, most of them I would say will be spurious.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=263)** That is, they are statistically present, but it's accidental that there's no direct causal relationship between the two things.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=273)** Also, especially when there are spurious associations, but even when there's a strong causal association, you can find that changes in the context or changes in the population can cause the model to work differently.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=287)** It doesn't predict as well and so you have to be more sensitive to these changes.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=292)** And then finally, no matter what kind of analysis you do or what kind of outcome you're interested in, it's important to iterate, to test, to adapt, to repeat, to make sure that your model adapts well to these new circumstances.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=306)** But with those issues in mind, there's so much work in predictive models, both the very basic, simple kind you can do in a spreadsheet and the kind that requires super computers, that can be applied directly to medical and healthcare questions.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/predicting-outcomes?u=76281980&t=320)** I encourage you to spend a little time to see what methods have been applied most usually to the topics that are of greatest interest to you and the work that you do.

#### Treatments and ROI
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980)

> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=17)** Also, my brain is central to how I make a living.

>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=39)** It's an example of the saying: An ounce of prevention is worth a pound of cure.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=44)** Keeping things from happening in the first place can be a lot easier, cheaper, and more effective than trying to fix the damage afterwards.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=52)** Similarly, when it comes to medical treatments, if two treatments are equally effective at treating a disease, most people would prefer the one that's more cost effective in addition to being medically effective.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=66)** And this is where the concept of return on investment or ROI can be useful in healthcare, medicine and public health.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=74)** It's a basic idea from the business world.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=76)** You want to make choices that will give you good results for the time, effort and money you put into them.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=82)** It's a general principle, so it doesn't apply to just economics.
>

>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, let me be clear about just a few things before I say anything else.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=104)** I am aware medical decisions involve more than economics.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=108)** That's not the only factor that goes into it.
>

>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=122)** Also, this kind of decision-making works best at the macro-level.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=127)** It works better for populations than for individuals who have idiosyncratic situations.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=134)** It becomes more complex there.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=136)** So, again, we're talking about something that can help inform decisions based on whatever metric is most important to you, and it works best at the group level or population level.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=148)** So please keep those things in mind.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=150)** Now, as I mentioned earlier in another video, researchers can use any of several different measures when looking at the health of populations.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=158)** These include mortality, whether a person is dead or alive, YLD, which is years lost to disability.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=166)** So, for instance, you may be on a respirator, and you are alive, but it's inconvenient, and it creates its own problems.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=176)** Or DALY, that's the disability adjusted life years.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=180)** Again, these measures are attempts to quantify the wellness or impaired wellness of large groups as opposed to individuals, but that's also at the group level where much of the decision-making can take place.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=196)** The principle of getting return on investment for health decisions is actually simple, at least in theory.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=204)** Number one is you want to compare.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=206)** You want to take two or more possible interventions, and then you want to look at the cost or the time to implement, or the number of people who can be served.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=218)** Any number of things that are used as the input, what you have to do to make these different interventions happen.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=227)** And then you can compare the population measures, like mortality, YLD or DALY, as outcomes or something else that's going to be relevant to the situation that you're working with.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=239)** So you set up the comparison and that gives you the raw data that you need to work with.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=245)** But after that, you need to contextualize.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=248)** You compare the effects for different populations, and those probably include age, gender.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=256)** They may include race or ethnicity.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=258)** They may include urban and rural or other factors that are important to the groups that you're working with.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=264)** You may also want to look at things like preexisting medical conditions, or family risk factors or other things that can influence a patient's responsiveness to a particular kind of treatment.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=277)** Once you do the analysis and you put those results into this kind of context, you have something that can help you with data-informed decision-making.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=288)** Again, the idea is the data can be analyzed with any number of strategies.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=292)** Maybe use something simple, like a contingency table or something more advanced like multi-level regression or even random forests.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=299)** Those analyses can then help inform.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=302)** Again, I want to emphasize that they inform, not determine the decisions of medical professionals and public health agencies.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=310)** Ideally, that process can help them ensure better health and wellness for a broader, more diverse range of a greater number of people than they could do otherwise.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/treatments-and-roi?u=76281980&t=320)** In healthcare, as in so many other areas, a little data can go a long way towards helping to make wise, effective and compassionate decisions.

### 4. Patient Care

#### Selecting treatments and interventions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Treating patients is, of course, at the center of what doctors, nurses and medical professionals do and aside from the enormous skill and experience needed to carry out the treatment successfully, there's the issue of simply deciding what you're going to do in the first place, that is selecting what treatment or intervention should be used.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=21)** There's always more than one way to treat a given illness or injury, and a huge range of factors play into the decision of which approach to take.

>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=40)** And fortunately, classification is one of the things that data science does best.

>

>

>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=235)** You can also do random forests, a ensemble of decision trees.
>

>

>

>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=263)** These or many others to making selections of treatments.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=268)** And in terms of the output, there're several different ways to do this as well.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=273)** You can ask the algorithm to produce the single best approach based on the weighted data.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=279)** So this how much we care about the risk of side effects.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=283)** This is how much we care about the time to recovery.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=286)** And you put those on there, it can say well, based on that, this is probably the single best method.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=291)** That's used for robo-advisors, it's used for marketing strategies or you can look at several different methods and you can get a scored outcome, a probability of a good outcome for each of those different methods.
>

> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/selecting-treatments-and-interventions?u=76281980&t=327)** It's important to remember that human in the loop decision making, a term used in many other fields of data science is absolutely critical to medical procedures and selecting treatments but that so many of the methods, again where I've given you just general outlines can be used productively in something as consequential as the selection of treatment for a patient.

#### Individualized treatment
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's begin our discussion of personalized medicine with a little line from Anton Chekhov, the Russian playwright, who in 1903 said, "If many remedies are prescribed for an illness, you may be certain that the illness has no cure."
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=15)** That's a rather depressing line from his play "The Cherry Orchard."
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=20)** The idea is that when something works obviously and works well, like putting a cast on a broken arm, then everybody figures out that's the way to go.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=30)** On the other hand, there are medical problems which have no established solution.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=35)** For example, think of the early days at COVID before the vaccines were developed, but there were plenty of others in which people propose all sorts of solutions.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=44)** Some of which may seem reasonable, in fact, may pan out and some of which are complete nonsense.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=50)** Then again, it's important to remember, we're not all the same, and it's also well established that certain treatments vary in their effectiveness according to a number of variables that should be taken into consideration by medical professionals.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=65)** So, for instance, things like age or a person's lifestyle.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=69)** Are they sitting on their sofa and playing their phone all the time?
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=72)** Or other existing health concerns, any of these and a whole host of others are going to affect whether a treatment is likely to be successful and the best way to go about treating a particular disorder or illness.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=86)** And if you want to get really particular, you can get as specific as individual genetics, the things that make each one of us who we are.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=98)** Now, when medicine goes to this level, it's known by a few different names.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=102)** It can be called personalized medicine or precision medicine, stratified medicine, or P4 medicine, where P4 stands for predictive, preventive, personalized and participatory.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=117)** The idea is that you're going to detect and prevent disease through the use of extensive biomarker testing, through close monitoring, through deep statistical analysis and through patient health coaching, really a very comprehensive approach that also involves a lot of data.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=132)** And one of the interesting places where this data can show up is in another closely related field called pharmacogenomics.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=140)** This is the study of how a person's unique genetic makeup, their genome, influences his or her response to various medications.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=149)** So to give an example, in cancer treatments, there are actually two genomes that may influence prescribing decisions.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=158)** There's the genome of the person who has the cancer, that's the germline genome, or the genes that a person was born with, and there's also the somatic genome, which is the genome of the cancerous or malignant tumor.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=172)** So it's possible to extract samples from both of those to get the genome and to see how that can influence the decisions.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=181)** And that's important because genes affect a lot of different things.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=184)** They affect the risk of side effects.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=186)** They affect the likely benefit from treatment.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=189)** They can affect the dose or the level of the dose needed for therapeutic response.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=193)** They can also affect the optimal duration of treatment; how long should you continue?
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=199)** Now, the nice thing about this is you're not starting from scratch all the time.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=202)** There are pharmacogenomic biomarkers.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=205)** So, for example, the US Food and Drug Administration, the FDA, provides a table of pharmacogenomic biomarkers in drug labeling, it's available on the web, that at this exact moment lists 499 biomarkers for 341 drugs.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=222)** About half of those are for treating cancer, but they also include things like neurology, psychiatry, hematology, rheumatology, and others.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=230)** And so if you have information about the patient's genome, and if you have some information possibly about the genome of the tumor or other illness itself, you might be able to line that up with some of this known information about which medications work well with which genomes.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=250)** Now, there is as always a risk/benefit or a cost/benefit approach to this.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=256)** Genomic testing can be very expensive, and you do run the risk of overconfidence and even overtreatment, doing more than you should, because you feel you have this very, very fine guided approach.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=272)** Some people have talked about the risk of over-testing, over-diagnosis, overtreatment and overcharging in precision medicine.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=279)** But the work is progressing exponentially and shows enormous potential in terms of taking the genomic data of each individual and comparing that to medications, to selecting an approach that'll be ideally suited to that one individual.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/individualized-treatment?u=76281980&t=294)** That's the promise of data science in medicine.

>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=62)** And so I'm scared of it.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=64)** I love Zion.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=66)** I love going in the park, but I don't want to do the change at Angels Landing anymore.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=70)** On the other hand, I think that's a realistic fear.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=72)** I don't think it's an example of acrophobia, which is an irrational and kind of paralyzing fear of heights, even when you're not actually in a, you know, objectively dangerous situation.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=85)** But a lot of people do face irrational fears about things like heights and insects and enclosed spaces, lots of different things.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=94)** And instead of putting people in a situation, immersion therapy, where they're going to experience it, it can be completely overwhelming.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=102)** We can have virtual reality come to the rescue.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=106)** You don't need to go there in person.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=107)** You don't need to put yourself in that risky situation.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=110)** You can go there virtually.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=112)** And this has been one of the more interesting applications of virtual reality in healthcare.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=117)** So for example, there's exposure therapy.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=119)** So as I mentioned, acrophobia, you don't have to put a person in an actual high place.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=124)** You can have them put on the VR goggles and see what it would be like to be in a high place, or even just a video game image of a person in a high place or things like fear of spiders and mice.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=134)** One of the more interesting applications has been the treatment of post traumatic stress disorder, PTSD for veterans, by putting them into a video game simulation of the situation that provokes the PTSD response.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=149)** And by gradually allowing them to experience it in a situation where they know they are physically not going to get hurt, but it can help them recover from some of the trauma of those episodes.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=161)** So that's one great application of VR in healthcare is a safe and controlled version of exposure therapy.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=169)** Another interesting one is helping people with social anxiety learn to work with social interaction.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=175)** Now it can happen both with anxiety and you, for instance, have a job interview or you have a conversation with somebody, but also sometimes for people with autism, it can be a way of getting safe interactions and learning some of the skills.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=191)** For people who are transgender, it can be an opportunity to see the world in the way that better fits with their identity.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=199)** And so these are all ways that virtual reality can help ease some of the stressful situations.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=205)** Another really interesting one is helping healthcare providers and really anybody who has to deal with somebody who has an illness, helping them develop empathy.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=216)** So by putting on the VR goggles, you can start to see what it's like to have symptoms of traumatic brain injuries, to have physical limitations, to experience some forms of dementia.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=226)** And by getting a better first person view of some of these limitations, the people who live with these people, the people who take care of them can get a better understanding of what they're dealing with and that can inform their care.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=241)** Now one more really interesting possibility is surgical VR or virtual reality for surgery.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=249)** Now this is very well suited for minimally invasive procedures, where what you have is very close to a version of VR anyhow, you are using a scope that goes into a very small incision.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=259)** You are seeing what's going on there.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=260)** Now that's basically a direct camera feed, but it can be done in a virtual reality format.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=266)** So for instance, it can allow the overlay of diagnostic data.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=271)** It can allow the surgeon to switch between different views, between the MRI of the injury, between the augmented reality, which is identifying parts or giving measurements or giving the medical history of the patient or an unprocessed image, the unvarnished, as though it were simply a straight camera.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=288)** There is a lot of possibility for this, in part because minimally invasive procedures are common.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=294)** And by ramping these up to include elements of virtual reality, it dramatically expands the potential reach of VR within healthcare.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=305)** And so when taken with things like the safe contained version of exposure therapy, the ability to experience stressful situations, or try to experience a social reality that you might not be able to otherwise or developing empathy for others.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/virtual-reality-in-healthcare?u=76281980&t=321)** All of these are interesting applications of virtual reality within healthcare and things that data science can help improve and expand and make more useful to healthcare providers around the world.

### 5. Self-Care

#### Wearables and health monitoring
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] There's a joke in the exercise world that goes, "Strava or it didn't happen."
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=5)** The idea is that when you go cycling or running or swimming or whatever you do, you need to record your activity and upload it to an exercise tracking in social network site, like Strava, so other people can see it and they can get all the data and compare themselves with each other.

> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=22)** If you don't record or upload your activity, like the very sad time, I rode up the very steep seven mile canyon by my house without pressing start on my cycling computer, it's like it never happened.

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=111)** And then there's some interesting developments in specialized devices.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=114)** Those include what are called smart patches, which are usually prescribed by a doctor and they adhere to your skin, but also socks and gloves with things, for instance, that they can measure, whether somebody who's in the hospital, who's not supposed to be up and walking around, is in fact up and walking around and it can automatically notify the medical staff so they can come and check on the person.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=135)** But there's a whole host of other things, including straps for monitoring pregnancy and fetal heart rate.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=140)** There are so many different ways that you can gather data and that you can do this on your own.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=147)** Now some of the measurements can include things like the number of steps walked and the number of floors climbed, the heart rate, the blood oxygen saturation, that's the SpO2, the calories burned, blood pressure and time spent exercising.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=162)** Any one of these can go into it.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=164)** On the other hand, there are also inferences.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=167)** So the device your monitor takes some of the basic information.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=171)** And then by using some of its algorithms, it concludes that other things are happening.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=176)** Like for instance, whether you're possibly experiencing some symptoms of COVID-19, having to do with oxygen levels and heart rate.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=184)** Also, assessing sleep quality or stress and mood.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=188)** It can infer some of the symptoms of fertility.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=190)** Also, alert people to possible seizures and possibly get automated help or even assessing something like frailty.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=199)** Now, these are all great.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=200)** One of the most interesting applications that I've seen actually has to do with glucose monitoring and insulin patches.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=208)** So this is for diabetes.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=209)** While people have been able to monitor their glucose levels with finger stick tests for some time, and they still do that because it's a very accurate way of measuring things.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=219)** It's also not very convenient, especially when you're traveling around during the day.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=224)** So now it's possible to have monitors that are built into smart patches that you wear on your skin, like you see here.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=230)** These can either be flash glucose monitors, or FGMs, which take a measurement when they're triggered, say, by holding your phone up against it, or they can be continuous glucose monitors or CGMs that automatically measure glucose at regular intervals, say, one minute or 15 minutes.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=248)** And they can sound alarms that the values go out of a safe range.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=251)** In fact, it's even possible for the patches to administer insulin automatically, which makes an enormous difference for people who are living with diabetes.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=261)** In addition, there are, as I mentioned earlier, pregnancy and fetal monitoring, which is a great way for expectant mothers to monitor their health and their baby's health.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=272)** There are also ways of monitoring chronic pain and joint movements.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=276)** Say, for instance, if you've been injured, if you've had surgery, and as you go through physical therapy, you can get the feedback on your own progress or even postsurgical cardiac monitoring for people who've recently had heart attacks that this is a great way of checking what's going on.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/wearables-and-health-monitoring?u=76281980&t=292)** But what all of these have in common is that they are ways that individual people, you and me can monitor our own health, get feedback through our watch, through our smartphone, through some other device that lets us know how well our body is functioning, give us the feedback we need and hopefully guide us in the right direction that we can be healthier and happier through the integration of data science and healthcare.

#### Self-serve healthcare

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=9)** That kind of service felt like a luxury, that or you live in Oregon, New Jersey, South Africa, Indonesia, or Egypt where attendants are still common or even mandatory, but definitely not in Utah like I do.

>

>

>

>

>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=167)** Not surprisingly, they also found that patient satisfaction went up by 20%, which is a huge change, a tremendous boost for the organization.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=178)** And then there's less work.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=180)** So for example, automated check-ins via kiosk or even via cellphone location can reduce unnecessary face-to-face check-ins by over 50%, which then allows staff to focus on more complicated questions that actually require human intervention.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=195)** And then more payments.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=197)** When patients are able to book their own appointments online and check-in online, they are also more likely to make their payments upfront, which reduces the need for the provider to chase down the patient or their insurance company to get payments, because it turns out that usually several steps are necessary.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=214)** Getting it taken care of automatically and upfront is a great benefit.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=219)** And then there's the issue of more and better data.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=223)** Self-service systems make it easier to gather and to use data from patients.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=228)** In fact, patient reported outcomes, also known as PROs, are considered the gold standard in data collection and using digital services makes it much, much easier to share that data accurately in a timely manner.
>

>

>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=263)** Hopefully, it reduces the number of times it has to be filled out and it gets the information to the right person in the right time.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=270)** The overall moral of all of this is that convenience wins.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=273)** A 2019 NRC Health Report found that 80% of patients would switch providers for convenience factors alone and that was before the pandemic that changed everything to make these factors even more important.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=287)** Now it is something that you simply cannot ignore.

>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=297)** And as a patient, it makes our lives so much easier.

> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/self-serve-healthcare?u=76281980&t=301)** It makes us more likely to engage with your practice in the future.

#### Patient experience
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Many medical professionals understandably focus on their patients' medical problems.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=6)** But there are many good reasons to make the experience an overall positive one for patients.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=12)** First off, it's good manners, and of course, insurance companies pay attention to things like patient satisfaction too.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=19)** So if you're a medical professional, you probably know the CAHPS Survey.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=24)** That stands for Consumer Assessment of Healthcare Providers and Systems.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=27)** It's a very common method of assessing patient experience.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=31)** It was developed by the Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=35)** That's the AHRQ here in the US.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=37)** But aside from the specter of institutional assessment, there are other reasons to focus on positive patients' experience.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=46)** And we can think of some of these as the carrot and the stick.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=49)** Let's think about the carrot, the positive things first.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=53)** The first one is improved self management.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=56)** Research shows that patients who report positive interactions with their providers also demonstrate greater self-management skills and quality of life.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=65)** So they're going to take care of themselves better.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=68)** That's a good thing.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=69)** Second, adherence to treatment.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=72)** Patients who have good experiences with their providers, especially those with good communication with their providers, are more likely to adhere to their treatment plans.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=80)** And that's especially important for patients with chronic conditions, because they're going to be working on this for a very long time and seeing the provider many times for the same thing.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=89)** So the communication actually is directly associated with improved adherence and better health.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=96)** And in fact, the third step is better health outcomes itself.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=99)** Patients who report positive interactions also have better health outcomes measured a full year after discharge.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=106)** So it has a long term consequence.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=109)** These are all great outcomes, and they are things that any doctor would be happy to see in their patients.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=113)** And of course, patients would be thrilled to have all these things as well.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=117)** And then, there's the negative stuff, the stick, reasons why you better do it, or there can be consequences.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=124)** Number one is increased patient churn.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=127)** Lower patient satisfaction is not surprisingly associated with patients picking up and leaving to go see somebody else.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=135)** Building a practice is hard.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=137)** So you don't want to lose the people you already have.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=140)** In the marketing world, this kind of customer loss is referred to as churn, and it's a bad thing.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=145)** In fact, one study found that patients who reported the poorest quality relationships with their providers were three times more likely to deliberately leave than were patients with the highest quality relationships.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=157)** So it makes a difference in terms of keeping the people who allow you to do your practice.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=165)** Second one is increased employee turnover.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=169)** Happy patients make for happy medical staff and the opposite, grumpy patients make for grumpy medical staff is true as well.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=177)** Keep everybody happy.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=179)** One hospital reported that their efforts to increase patient satisfaction reduced employee turnover by nearly 5%.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=188)** It's kind of amazing to think that an intervention on one entirely different group of people would have a noticeable, measurable effect on a different group.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=198)** So that's a great effect.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=200)** You want to keep your staff, you want to keep 'em happy, so making your patients happy is a good way to do that.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=205)** And then, three, probably the scariest one for doctors is increased malpractice risks.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=211)** It's the long arm of the law.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=213)** One study found that for every step down the five point scale from very good to very poor was associated with over a 20% increase in the risk of malpractice lawsuits.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=227)** Lawsuits, of course, are horrible.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=229)** So it behooves medical providers to take the sensible approach of keeping people happy.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=233)** Again, it's a win-win situation.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=235)** And so the question may be, what should you do?
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=238)** Well, most of the stuff is kind of really embarrassingly obvious and I don't really like to have to bring it up in a data sense situation, but I will connect the two in a moment.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=248)** Number one, say hello.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=250)** Greet your patient by name.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=253)** Introduce yourself and your role.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=255)** If the person had to wait for a long time, and that happens sometimes, acknowledge that, apologize for it.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=261)** It's a human kindness.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=263)** Also, acknowledge any concerns that they may have, what brought them in, how it's affecting them.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=268)** Try to listen without interruption.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=271)** Again, I know all of this sounds really obvious, but I'm sure that all of us have also had experiences of going to see some medical provider and at least some of these things not happening.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=282)** You feel like an object.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=283)** You feel alien.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=285)** A few more suggestions.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=287)** It's helpful to sit, so you don't appear rushed.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=290)** Let the person know they're going to get more than 30 seconds of your time.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=294)** Explain the time that is needed for the evaluation to be conducted and for the diagnostic results to be returned.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=302)** Some of them might happen right away.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=303)** Some of them could take a few days.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=305)** Make that clear upfront.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=306)** Also, if there any delays that you can anticipate upfront, let your patients know, so they have a reasonable idea.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=314)** It's like you order something from online.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=317)** If there is a shipping delay, they let you know.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=319)** It's nice.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=321)** Also, address the patient's comfort.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=323)** What's going to be easy for them.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=325)** If there's something that is not comfortable but necessary, explain that.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=329)** And then, when you're all done, provide clear discharge instructions and confirm their understanding of the home care plan.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=337)** So these are all pretty obvious things, but if you're a medical provider, you might actually consider making a little checklist and practicing these things because they all have a meaningful effect on patient satisfaction.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=352)** Now, that list, I didn't make that list up.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=355)** That comes from actual medical research.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=357)** I'm going to add a few things of my own.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=359)** Number one, if there is some way for you to tell people where to park the car, now, I've been to the medical clinic in Zion National Park.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=367)** It's just a double wide trailer.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=369)** So these suggestions don't apply to them, but pretty much everywhere else where it's a giant high-rise building or spread across six or seven or 10 different buildings, these things are important.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=380)** Where should they park?
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=381)** So they don't have to walk really far if they're hurting or in a hurry.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=386)** Also, where to enter the building, how to find your office in the labs, anything you can do to reduce that sense of feeling like I've messed up or I'm late, or it really hurts for me to walk, I wish I didn't have to go back and forth, is going to make the patient's life easier.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=402)** And then if the patient's going to be there for any amount of time, or if they're waiting for somebody who is getting an MRI or something, let them know where they can get some snacks.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=410)** Let 'em know where restroom is.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=411)** Make the experience more comfortable for them.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=415)** And so, again, this is not rocket science.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=418)** This is pretty easy stuff, but is designed to make the experience less painful, more pleasant for the patient and for anybody who's coming with them.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=428)** Now, specifically, in terms of how data science works into this, well, it can be involved in an awful lot of these steps, from reducing the need for repetitive forms, I mean, I really like to think that if I fill out an outcome questionnaire once, I'm not going to have to do it again for somebody else the next day.
>
> **[7:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=443)** It should be in the system, to giving medical providers more efficient summaries, so they can focus on their patients.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=450)** If they don't have to spend 15 minutes, if they can get information they need in 90 seconds, that's going to be extra time that they can spend on their patients, to assessing patient satisfaction, not just through standardized surveys that are administered by the providers, but through online ratings on multiple different sites or through social media posts where people may be grumpy or may say actually something nice about their doctor online.
>
> **[7:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=476)** The flexibility and efficiency of data science methods can make these things possible and can help create a win-win situation, both for medical providers and for their patients.
>
> **[8:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=487)** So, if you are in the medical field, get familiar with data science and find out how you can better gather this data and make better use of it.
>
> **[8:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/patient-experience?u=76281980&t=496)** Everybody will be happy for it.
