### 6. Medical Administration and Insurance

#### Administrative burden for doctors and staff
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Perhaps it's time to take a short break for a horror story here in this course, except instead of being about monsters and serial killers, this one's about paperwork and administrative burden, which is kind of the same thing.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=15)** The idea here is administrative burden is really anything that adds non-care-related costs to healthcare.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=24)** And it can include things like scheduling or obtaining and sharing medical records, or getting prior authorizations or resolving billing issues, or resolving premium problems.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=37)** There's a lot of things that go into this.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=39)** And in the United States at least, they add anywhere between 15 and 30% to the total cost of healthcare in the country, so this is a very significant part of the entire healthcare equation.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=52)** And part of this is due to the shockingly common use in medical settings and legal offices of that leftover dinosaur, the 1980s technology, the fax machine.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=64)** Many medical offices still transfer records by fax, which in case you haven't looked at one in the past 30 years, are horribly low resolution.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=72)** Although maybe it creates its own kind of encryption system because of a fax of a fax is essentially unreadable.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=79)** But the frustrating part is that somebody has to take that nightmare of a document and then manually enter the information into their own record system.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=89)** And so the administrative burden simply accumulates.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=95)** And there are some important consequences of this.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=97)** For example, 62% of physicians enter the same data into multiple systems, and that's ridiculous.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=105)** You should be able to enter it once and have it automatically propagate to where it needs to be.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=110)** And the flip side of that is 68% of physicians have to navigate multiple systems to find the needed information.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=116)** So in terms of sending it out and bringing it back in, there's terrible redundancy.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=123)** And from the patient side, 24% of patients have reported that they have delayed or even foregone medical care as a result of their own burden, the extra paperwork, and really just the pain that surrounds the whole process.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=140)** Fortunately, there are a few ways that data science can help with this.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=144)** Number one is secure data sharing.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=147)** Now the reason offices use fax is because it's harder to share data.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=152)** And when you're trying to keep data secure, you don't want to have it so people can just hit forward and give a perfect copy to somebody else.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=159)** So faxes actually have an advantage of being harder to share, but on the other hand, there have been developments in the last 40 years with better ways to share data between health providers and to integrate the data from multiple systems and generally make the system less of a horrifying maze.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=180)** Also, data science is good at natural language processing, or NLP.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=185)** Medical professionals spend an inordinate amount of time transcribing notes and data.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=190)** That's part of the accountability and transparency, which everybody agrees is a good thing in principle.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=196)** But anything that can make that process easier, less time consuming, less stressful, is going to be good.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=202)** Ideally, it would be integrated into their caregiving activities so they don't have to then go find a dark corner of the office for an hour a day trying to recreate everything that happened for the record keeping process.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=214)** And then unstructured data analysis, the ability to analyze unstructured data.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=220)** That means data that's not in the nice rows and columns of a spreadsheet or a database.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=225)** That's critical because up to 80% of all medical data falls into this unstructured data category.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=232)** Obviously, it's a hard task, which is why it hasn't been taken on so much, but that is also one of the things that data science does best, taking this difficult, unwieldy data and working with it in a way that transforms it to make it easier to get useful insights there.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=252)** So if you're looking for an opportunity to use data science productively in healthcare, one of the best places to go is to specifically target the administrative burden and start looking at things like data sharing, natural language processing and the unstructured data analysis.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/administrative-burden-for-doctors-and-staff?u=76281980&t=271)** You'll get a huge benefit, both for the providers and for the patients.

#### Blockchain and medical data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Given that this course is about data science and how it can be used in healthcare, it seems important to talk about the potential of blockchain in healthcare, but the keyword there is potential.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=12)** The reason for that is that most of the arguments about blockchain used in healthcare are still at the theoretical or very early research stages.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=23)** There is, however, one interesting exception to that, and it's the Baltic country of Estonia.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=28)** Although it looks like a fairytale land here in the old town of Tallinn, Estonia has its tech up and running, which is one reason that they're the headquarters of the European Union's IT agency.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=40)** For example, Estonia started using the blockchain for health records in 2012.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=45)** And now 95% of all health data there is stored using blockchain, and so is 99% of prescription data.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=51)** So it is possible.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=54)** Now there are few potential advantages of blockchain that are commonly mentioned.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=60)** Number one is it's decentralized, that it's not that there's a single server that has all the data.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=65)** It's spread across, it's distributed across many, many, many, many different nodes.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=70)** The advantage there is that no one person can restrict access to it and that it makes it possible for other people to get to it.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=78)** That can be a great convenience.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=82)** The second one is that it's verifiable.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=85)** The whole point of the blockchain is that it builds this single comprehensive record, and it makes it possible to verify transactions in a historical chain, which also makes it really easy, in theory, to do things like store patient records and to store the test results in a way that you can be confident about their accuracy.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=109)** The third one is that blockchain is immutable.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=112)** Once it's written, once it's in there, it stays there, and it can't be changed.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=117)** And so there are potentially huge advantages to that when it comes to health records and other sensitive data that you don't have to worry about it getting corrupted.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=128)** Now, those are the main points about blockchain, the decentralized, verifiable, and immutable.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=135)** Those form the core of the argument for blockchain in general, and some of its potential application in healthcare.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=142)** So let's think about the potential in healthcare, specifically.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=146)** It could be used to keep data private.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=149)** It also can facilitate the sharing of data because it's not housed on any one-person server, but it's out there distributed in a decentralized manner, which makes it equally accessible to everybody.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=162)** And because of its consistency, it can help avoid miscommunication, 'cause it's not a matter of person A packaging the information and then sending it to person B, but it's there in a single common ledger that everybody can look at at once.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=179)** It potentially allows people to verify the supply chain for things like the production of pharmaceuticals, as well as verify consent from the patient for sharing data.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=189)** Again, that's one place where it's often done by fax machines, you write a paper, you send it, it gets faxed to one place, but it can be done in this location.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=198)** On the other hand, there are some major challenges with blockchain.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=201)** And first off is, there are variations in blockchain.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=204)** I mean the term blockchain is an umbrella term for a host of related technologies.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=209)** In healthcare, for example, researchers are currently exploring several different forms from permission to blockchains and off-chain storage to tethered blockchains.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=220)** There's a lot of active work being done.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=223)** So same blockchain is a very general thing.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=227)** It needs to get more specific to actually be implemented.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=230)** The second one, which really needs to be in shining letters, is regulations.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=236)** Health data is very tightly regulated.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=239)** In the US, for example, there are stringent requirements that come from HIPAA and other sources.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=245)** Given the decentralized nature of blockchain, it's hard to know exactly how those regulations would be applied and the immutable nature.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=256)** So once something's in the blockchain, it can't be changed.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=259)** Well, that's often a very good thing, but if there's a mistake or if there is a violation of privacy that's built into the blockchain, well, we potentially have some issues.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=267)** So it's not necessarily clear how the relevant regulations would be met.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=274)** Although, I'll say, that's not an insurmountable problem.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=277)** It's an area of ongoing work.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=279)** Next, there are the well known environmental costs of at least some forms of blockchain.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=285)** So common forms of blockchain, like for instance, those use for cryptocurrencies, can have staggering energy demands that make them environmentally unsustainable.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=294)** On the other hand, there are variations that are less common, but are also much less energy intensive.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=301)** Obviously, given that we're concerned about health, it would be good to store and access health records in an environmentally-responsible way.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=310)** And then the last thing is trust.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=312)** The biggest challenge isn't technical, but it's really sort of psychological and social.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=318)** Blockchain, first off is just hard to understand, the technology is complicated.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=322)** And specific implementations can get enormously byzantine, but there have been a number of strange things that have happened with cryptocurrencies, like thefts or disappearing money or associations with illicit activities and wild fluctuations in currencies that were specifically designed to be stable that make many people outside of the tech world, and even many in it, wary of the technology in general.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=351)** However, it's reasonable to say that as more people come to use blockchain technologies in more ways and become more familiar with it and, importantly, have positive experiences with it, it will become possible to build up the trust necessary for its full implementation in healthcare.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=371)** The paradox there is that the blockchain is actually described as zero-trust solution, which means you can verify the data and you don't have to take somebody else's word for it that it's accurate, but that's a different issue from getting people to use it in the first place.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/blockchain-and-medical-data?u=76281980&t=383)** And so this larger social issue of building trust and confidence, as well as overcoming some of these major hurdles in terms of regulation and energy usage will be important parts of solving the blockchain puzzle to see what kind of value it really is able to bring to storing healthcare records.

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=7)** Something that may be a necessary evil in certain cases, but hopefully for just other people, and something you'd really rather not acknowledge its existence at all, but to state the painfully obvious, healthcare costs money.

>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=73)** That's unbelievable, nearly 80% of that, from their estimates, comes from the use of wearable AI applications, and roughly 10% each from AI applications in health monitoring and in medical imaging.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=89)** But if you're an AI developer, you can do some great work that can save lives directly.
>

>

>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=106)** Also, early identification of health risks and targeted intervention can save over $100,000 per patient for expensive procedures.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=117)** So identifying things that you shouldn't be spending money on through fraud, and avoiding that can save billions of dollars, and also identifying people at risk, and treating them sooner, while it is easier and more manageable, and the problems are less severe, is also going to, not just make that person's life a lot better, but also save a lot of money in the healthcare system.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=142)** So, how can data science help in insurance?

>

>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=182)** But other ones there, again, things like risk assessment and claims prediction are going to be very, very specific to the insurance industry, and not just the marketing of the insurance in general.

>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=201)** Preterm labor, or having your baby prematurely is risky for the health of both the mothers, and of course, for the babies.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=211)** And one group of researchers who was working on this used very sophisticated methods and they could develop models that could predict preterm birth when the mother was about 28 weeks along.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=223)** So what that means is that 28 weeks into the pregnancy, they were able to identify which babies were likely to be born prematurely, and they could then start doing a medical intervention for that child and mother.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=237)** On the other hand, the researchers found that by additional work, improved modeling, more data, they could make predictions with a similar level of accuracy at 20 weeks, that's eight weeks sooner.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=249)** And that makes it possible to do your intervention before things have started escalating, and to have a greater impact on health.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=258)** So a predictive model that is able to predict sooner is able to save lives, and save money.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=266)** That's going to be a huge part of any insurance company's interests, and any healthcare system's interests.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=273)** Or another one, developing predictive models for diseases that develop over time, allowing earlier targeting, like we had with the risk of preterm delivery.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=284)** So things like diabetes, which develops over time, or chronic kidney disease, or substance abuse disorders, or depression, and other mood disorders, they don't happen all at once, like getting in a car crash.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=298)** They are things that develop over time.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=300)** And so the ability of insurance companies to build models that can identify the risk, see it coming, that's one of the great advantages of predictive models in data science, see it coming, and if you can see just a little bit further, you can do interventions to help people be healthier sooner, and at the same time dramatically, reduce the cost of the associated care.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=327)** Now, theoretically, insurers can use data from a wide variety of sources to better understand health risks and the probability of claims.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=335)** They could potentially look at things like purchase history, transactional data on what you bought and where, maybe categorizing the food you bought as healthy and unhealthy, or data from wearables, smart watches, fitness trackers, other devices that monitor behaviors, and alert the wearer, or the insurance company, to early signs of illness.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=354)** Exterior, or external monitors.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=356)** So for instance, if your smartphone's connected to a machine at the gym, that information could also be used, and then social media, when a person posts about their physical or emotional health, that is information that is publicly available, it could potentially be used.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=372)** Now I will note of course, that all of these bring up sort of red flags about data ethics, which is why I'm going to talk about that separately very shortly.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=383)** But there is the issue that we know data like this is used in so many other marketing approaches.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=388)** And the question is whether it would be important to use it when looking at health related data also, from the insurer's point of view, from the healthcare provider's point of view, from the community's point of view, in terms of reducing the overall risk.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=403)** These are the sorts of things that data science has used productively in other domains, and so let's put them down as potential sources for information that has to do with risk, and the care of risk.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=417)** But the idea of all of this is that by using data science methods appropriately, then healthcare becomes both more efficient, more effective, and more cost effective, which makes it possible to provide better healthcare to a greater number of people.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-science-and-health-insurance?u=76281980&t=433)** And that is one of the great benefits of applying data science productively in healthcare and insurance.

### 7. Challenges

>

>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=154)** And consequently, you have impaired transparency, trust, and accountability.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=159)** And especially when it comes to medical decision-making, those three things, transparency, trust, and accountability are critical.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=166)** You're not going to use a method if you don't know what's going on and if you don't know who's going to be responsible if something goes wrong.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=172)** And so these are major issues.
>

>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=186)** And so they work on things called, for instance, trustworthy AI or interpretable AI, and as well as because we're using this in a medical context and it turns out that there are very special requirements for things like pharmaceuticals we understand, but medical devices and even software in medical situations has very special requirements.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=207)** And so there's also work at developing a clinical trials system for AI to make sure that it functions how it's supposed to and to better understand why it's making the decisions that it's making.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=219)** And so these are some of the limitations that are there in common versions, the most common versions by far of neural networks, but it turns out there's a lot to be done to make them really usable within the day-to-day context of healthcare and medicine.
>

> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=239)** For artificial intelligence algorithms to work well, they need large high-quality data sets.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=245)** I mean, really large.

>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=259)** That's very different from eCommerce, where all you're doing is you're counting the clicks and the ISP addresses on your website.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=265)** You can get millions of pieces of information an hour or so.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=270)** But with medical data, it's much, much more difficult.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=273)** Also there's the frustrating situation that electronic health care records, EHRs, which I've talked about before, they're are big improvement over having just random pieces of paper, but the problem is that the systems are frequently incompatible between one health provider and another, between a government office.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=293)** And so even though it's electronic and it is potentially shareable, there are still a lot of challenges in terms of being able to integrate it into a single algorithm.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=303)** Then there are the regulatory barriers, the compliance to the policies and laws.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=309)** Not surprisingly, when you're working in healthcare, there are very strong privacy regulations.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=315)** I've mentioned some of these like HIPAA.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=317)** And it also turns out that regulatory approval for medical technology, and that can include software and analyses, can take years.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=327)** If you're in the drug development business, you're playing a very long game and the same thing can be true about developing data science applications in healthcare.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=337)** And there's the very real concern about liability issues.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=341)** So if something goes wrong, that AI algorithm proposes that a person is healthy when they're not or proposes a treatment that doesn't work out and there ends up a bad outcome and there's a malpractice lawsuit, who is going to get sued?
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=357)** Who's on the hook? Is it the doctor?
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=360)** Is it the hospital?
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=361)** Is it the programmer who created the algorithm?
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=364)** And the sad thing is it's not clear yet.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=367)** We do not have settled case history to say who is responsible for when an algorithm makes a bad medical diagnosis or decision.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=378)** So until we get that figured out, there's going to be a lot of hesitation on the parts of medical professionals to really go all in on some of these algorithms.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=388)** And then finally, there's misaligned incentives and doctors may fear that AI will replace them.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=395)** For instance, a very prominent AI developer proposed many years ago that we just totally stop training radiologists because they're going to get completely replaced by AI.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=404)** Well, obviously that hasn't happened.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=407)** And so you can put that down as a overconfidence in the mechanism, but also it's the sort of thing that triggers the fear of a lot of doctors.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=417)** I'm a teacher, and I can tell you that everybody thinks that online teaching is going to make our jobs obsolete as well.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=424)** Or if you work in manufacturing, you worry about robotics replacing yours.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=427)** So many people have potential threats from technological developments either replacing them completely or diminishing their role.
>
> **[7:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=437)** And part of the data that supports this is this, that management-led versus doctor-led hospitals have higher AI use.
>
> **[7:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=446)** So when it's not a doctor but a professional manager, they make greater use of AI and data science.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=453)** But when it's a doctor, it's lower.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=455)** Now, one way to interpret that is that the doctors don't want to get replaced.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=460)** They don't want to have their light taken by the machines.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=464)** But another way to interpret that, and it's probably more likely, is that doctors may be more familiar with the challenges and the limitations, and they may understand why AI is not the best solution in a number of cases.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=478)** So at the moment, just this one little factoid that AI is used more often in hospitals led by management people as opposed to medical people, that's ambiguous in and of itself.
>
> **[8:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=492)** And so it becomes important to do a little more research to figure out what exactly is driving the changes in implementation.
>
> **[8:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=500)** If it's an issue of fear, there's things you can do about that.
>
> **[8:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=504)** If it's an issue that the doctors know that there are problems and limitations, then obviously it behooves the developers to address those issues directly.
>
> **[8:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=514)** The overall moral of this story is that unlike the famous line from Facebook founder, Mark Zuckerberg, when you're applying data science in healthcare, you can't move fast and break things.
>
> **[8:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=526)** People's lives are at stake.
>
> **[8:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=529)** And consequently, we have to be extremely cautious in how we develop things and how well they work, because it's not going to be the matter of a missed click or a missed sale.
>
> **[8:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=539)** It's going to be the matter of impairing somebody's life.
>
> **[9:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=543)** And so it's these extra issues that have made the adoption of data science and artificial intelligence slower in healthcare.
>
> **[9:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=551)** On the other hand, it doesn't mean it's never going to happen.
>
> **[9:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/limitations-and-barriers?u=76281980&t=553)** It simply means that we need to pay more attention and be more aware to the impact that these practices can have.

#### Data ethics in healthcare
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I hope it's obvious.
>

> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, it's important to keep a few things in mind.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=55)** For instance, one is HIPAA.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=57)** That's the United States Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=63)** It's the major law governing the privacy of medical records in the US.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=68)** Or, there's GDPR, that's the European Union's General Data Protection Regulation, and the UK has their own very similar version.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=78)** There's a CCPA/CPRA, that's the California Consumer Privacy Act.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=84)** And then the 2020 revision and expansion, the California Privacy Rights Act.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=91)** And then depending on which industry you're in, if you're doing medical research, if you're working in insurance, if you're doing some kind of promotion, then you're going to have industry-specific rules.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=102)** And so, whichever element you're working in, and if you're working in the United States, if you're working with customers from the EU, if you're working in California, these are collections of rules and laws that you're going to have to be familiar with.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=118)** Now, you can find more information on those and other courses here at LinkedIn Learning such as AI Accountability, but it's going to be incumbent upon you to make sure that your work meets these regulations and expectations so that you can do your work and have maximal impact.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=136)** Now, I can summarize a lot of this stuff in two very basic principles.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=140)** Number one is consent.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=142)** Back in the old days, meaning like 10 years ago, you could just grab data off the web and you could run with it.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=149)** There really wasn't any guidelines.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=152)** And these days, that doesn't work anymore.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=155)** You have to have people's consent in order to get their personal information.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=160)** And you can't do it passively.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=162)** They actually have to click a box that gives you permission to use their private data.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=169)** Also, there's privacy, that you don't share that data, you don't sell it without their permission, and if they want the data back, if they don't want you to have it anymore, they have recourse.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=179)** And so the data ultimately belongs to the consumer, to the people who generated it, and so you have to have their consent and you have to respect their autonomy in this one.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=189)** That's going to form the basis of most of the regulations of data use.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=195)** And in addition to that, there are some other issues that you would want to keep up with.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=199)** One is the very broad topic of justice and equity.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=204)** There are so many ways that justice and equity work into healthcare and into data science, in terms of differential health outcomes for people in different racial ethnic groups, and socioeconomic, and gender and sexuality.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=218)** It's an enormous topic, and it's something that even though you may think that you're there just dealing with the technical part, your technical work has a tremendous impact on that one.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=228)** And so you need to understand what the consequences, what the impacts of your work are.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=234)** Another very big issue is misinformation and disinformation.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=238)** Where misinformation is mistaken information, like rumors getting things wrong, and disinformation is by design, where people intentionally told false things.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=249)** As we have learned through the pandemic, misinformation and disinformation have a huge effect on healthcare.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=256)** Where, for instance, COVID vaccines may exist, but because of misinformation and disinformation, people are not willing to take them or get treatment.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=264)** I know several people who are nurses who have to actively fight with people just to put the oxygen on when they're at the hospital.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=272)** And when you do your data science work, one interesting thing is that there is a lot you can do to help combat misinformation and disinformation because most of it's spread through social media, and that's a place where data science is able to work extensively.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/data-ethics-in-healthcare-29602881?u=76281980&t=289)** Now, with those general things in mind, let's make a few recommendations.

>

> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/preparing-for-an-unknown-future?u=76281980&t=175)** And in data science, we can do the best to prepare for the kinds of problems we have seen, to anticipate ones that seem likely.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/preparing-for-an-unknown-future?u=76281980&t=183)** But really what it requires more than anything is that we be very, very flexible and that we be very open to learning as we go along and responding quickly.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/preparing-for-an-unknown-future?u=76281980&t=193)** That way we can still make the most of data science, to have an impact on what we're doing, which is making healthcare, medicine, and public health more effective, more efficient, and more accessible to more people.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/preparing-for-an-unknown-future?u=76281980&t=205)** The challenges we face may change and our exact methods for getting there may evolve but the goal always remains the same.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (4), find (2)
> **Code Keywords:** case. (1), public (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)


### 8. Continuing Your Data Science of Healthcare Learning Journey

#### Next steps and additional resources
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Barton] I'm glad that you've joined me for this course on data science in healthcare, medicine, and public health, and hopefully you've learned some really useful things.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=9)** On the other hand, this course was a map, it was just an overview of the territory covered by these topics, and maybe you want to know now where should you go next, now where?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=20)** Well, let me make a few recommendations, one is, if you were coming from the data science world and you were trying to learn more about healthcare, then I have some suggestions for you.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=32)** On the other hand, if you were coming from the healthcare world and you were trying to learn more about data science, again, it's going to be a slightly different journey, but for either one, I strongly recommend that you start by finding topics, see where the need is, what are people doing in the areas that are of greatest interest to you, read the news, read blogs, join data and health forums both on LinkedIn, Facebook, and other social media platforms, and then talk about it in person, discuss topics with others at meetups, and with conferences, and wherever you work.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=66)** So if you're a healthcare professional who wants to learn more about how you can use data science, I suggest you take a look at courses on these topics.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=72)** Number one, of course, is data science itself, learn more about the principles and practices of data science, both in general and how it's applied to specific domains.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=82)** You want to get up and running on a computer language, maybe Python, maybe R, maybe something else that is more common within your chosen field, but you need to have something that gives you flexibility and creativity in working with data, because that's where so much of the work is in data science.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=98)** In particular, we learned that 80% of the data in healthcare is unstructured and data scientists are well suited for that, so specifically focusing on how to work with unstructured data is going to be an important part of what you do, and then predictive modeling, both predicting about the future and predicting what other people would do if they were to work on it manually, right now that's sort of thinking prediction as automation.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=123)** These are areas where you can have a lot of impact if you are using data science as a healthcare professional.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=131)** On the other hand, if you're a data scientist who is curious about working in a health-related field, you may want to look into a few of these.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=137)** Number one is HIPAA regulations and other laws and policies that govern data.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=143)** It's different in healthcare than it is in other fields, and so you're going to want to make sure you understand exactly what you're dealing with so that you can work productively in that field.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=154)** Second, even though most doctors would agree is one of the most, you know, boring parts of their job, because you're working with data, you need to understand medical coding, both in terms of diagnoses and treatments, and understand the format in which the data will be coming to you.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=171)** And then third, of course, is industry standards, depending on what element of healthcare you're working in, maybe you're insurance, maybe you're in surgical care, you're going to have different standards for what kind of data you have and the kinds of conclusions that are going to be most useful to the practitioners in those fields.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=190)** I also strongly suggest that you get out there and mix it up in person, go to user groups, go to meet-up groups, talk to people one on one, you will learn new things, and you'll find ways that you can apply data science, you can take it to healthcare, that you can learn how to do new things in the medical world that you never thought were possible by connecting with others and seeing for yourself how data science can dramatically expand the effectiveness, the efficacy, and really, the insights of healthcare, medicine, and public health.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/the-data-science-of-healthcare-medicine-and-public-health/next-steps-and-additional-resources?u=76281980&t=224)** Thank you so much for joining me, and I can't wait to see what you are able to accomplish.
